Appendix C: Intrinsically Motivated Reinforcement Learning in RSP

Overview

This appendix recasts the Recursive Social Prediction (RSP) model in the language of reinforcement learning (RL). We show that valence functions as a reward signal, schemas as learned state representations, and Level 3 recursive prediction as model-based planning. The formulation bridges affective neuroscience and computational RL, revealing that the RSP hierarchy implements a form of unsupervised model-based reinforcement learning where the agent learns both the reward function and the state representation without external supervision.

Notation. All symbols from Appendix A are retained. New RL-specific notation is introduced in §C.1.

C.1 RL Notation and Mapping

C.1.1 New Symbols

C.1.2 RSP-to-RL Mapping

C.2 Valence as Reward

C.2.1 The Reward Signal

Symbol Domain Definition
\(r(t)\) \(\mathbb{R}\) Scalar reward signal at time \(t\)
\(\mathbf{s}^{\text{RL}}(t)\) \(\mathbb{R}^{n_s}\) RL state vector (agent's representation of its situation)
\(\pi(\mathbf{u} \mid \mathbf{s}^{\text{RL}})\) \([0,1]\) Policy: probability of action \(\mathbf{u}\) given state \(\mathbf{s}^{\text{RL}}\)
\(V(\mathbf{s}^{\text{RL}})\) \(\mathbb{R}\) Value function: expected cumulative future reward from state \(\mathbf{s}^{\text{RL}}\)
\(Q(\mathbf{s}^{\text{RL}}, \mathbf{u})\) \(\mathbb{R}\) Action-value function: expected return from taking action \(\mathbf{u}\) in state \(\mathbf{s}^{\text{RL}}\)
\(\delta(t)\) \(\mathbb{R}\) Temporal difference (TD) error
\(\gamma_{\text{RL}}\) \((0,1)\) Discount factor for future rewards
\(\mathcal{T}(\mathbf{s}' \mid \mathbf{s}^{\text{RL}}, \mathbf{u})\) \([0,1]\) Transition model: predicted next state given current state and action
\(\mathcal{R}(\mathbf{s}^{\text{RL}})\) \(\mathbb{R}\) Reward model: predicted reward from state \(\mathbf{s}^{\text{RL}}\)
RL Concept RSP Equivalent Explanation
Reward \(r(t)\) Valence \(v(t)\) Interoceptive prediction errors, signed by homeostatic direction
State \(\mathbf{s}^{\text{RL}}(t)\) Schema integration \(\mathbf{z}(t)\) Five-schema structured representation of the agent's situation
Action \(\mathbf{u}(t)\) Motor command + social behavior Both body-directed and socially-directed actions
Transition model \(\mathcal{T}\) Level 3 generative model \(g_3\) Predicts how schemas change given self-model and actions
Reward model \(\mathcal{R}\) Level 1 generative model \(g_1\) Predicts interoceptive (valence) consequences of schema states
Model-based planning Strange loop recursion Simulating social consequences before acting
Exploration Epistemic foraging Seeking states that reduce uncertainty about self and others
Value function \(V\) Expected free energy \(-G\) Negated expected free energy (states with low \(G\) are high-value)

In standard RL, reward is supplied externally. In the RSP model, reward is generated internally through interoceptive prediction:

$$r(t) = v(t) = \phi_v\big(\boldsymbol{\xi}_1(t)\big)$$

where \(\phi_v: \mathbb{R}^{n_i} \to [-1,1]\) projects the precision-weighted interoceptive prediction error onto the valence dimension. This projection is learned, not innate — but its inputs (interoceptive signals) are phylogenetically constrained.

Why this is unsupervised. No external agent specifies what counts as rewarding. The reward function emerges from the structure of the generative model:

  • Predictions consistent with homeostasis → small \(\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_1\) → low \(F_1\) → positive valence
  • Predictions violated by homeostatic disruption → large \(\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_1\) → high \(F_1\) → negative valence

The organism learns to associate certain schema configurations with positive or negative valence, creating a self-supervised reward landscape.

C.2.2 Reward Decomposition

The scalar reward decomposes into contributions from each schema:

$$r(t) = \sum_{k \in \mathcal{S}} w_k^r \cdot r_k(t) + r_{\text{cross}}(t)$$

where:

$$r_k(t) = -\frac{1}{2} \boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_{1,k}^T \boldsymbol{\Pi}_{1,k} \boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_{1,k}$$

is the schema-\(k\) contribution to reward (negative because large prediction errors reduce reward), \(w_k^r\) are learned reward weights, and \(r_{\text{cross}}\) captures cross-schema interactions.

C.2.3 Dopaminergic Implementation

Schema Reward Contribution Example
\(s_{\text{body}}\) Body-state comfort, pain absence Warmth, satiation, physical ease
\(s_{\text{spatial}}\) Environmental safety, navigational coherence Familiar surroundings, orientation
\(s_{\text{aff-hom}}\) Homeostatic balance Blood sugar stability, temperature regulation
\(s_{\text{attn}}\) Cognitive engagement, flow Focused attention, low distraction
\(s_{\text{self}}\) Agency, social belonging Competence, autonomy, relatedness

The temporal difference error in the RSP framework is:

$$\delta(t) = r(t+1) + \gamma_{\text{RL}} \cdot V(\mathbf{z}(t+1)) - V(\mathbf{z}(t))$$

This maps onto phasic dopamine firing in the ventral tegmental area (VTA) and substantia nigra, which signals the difference between received and expected reward. The RSP model predicts that dopaminergic TD errors should correlate with interoceptive prediction errors specifically — not just with external rewards — consistent with evidence that visceral and interoceptive signals modulate dopaminergic processing (Critchley & Harrison, 2013).

C.3 Schemas as Learned State Representations

C.3.1 The Representation Learning Problem

In RL, the choice of state representation \(\mathbf{s}^{\text{RL}}\) determines what the agent can learn and plan about. A poor representation makes learning impossible; a good one makes it efficient. The RSP model's five schemas are the agent's learned state representation:

$$\mathbf{s}^{\text{RL}}(t) = \mathbf{z}(t) = \text{Integrate}\big(\mathbf{a}(t), \{\mathbf{s}_k(t)\}_{k \in \mathcal{S}}\big)$$

This representation is learned through the same free energy minimization that drives perception and action (Appendix B). The schemas develop ontogenetically: body schema first (infancy), spatial schema next, affective homeostasis in parallel, attention as executive functions mature, and self-other distinction last (emerging with social cognition in toddlerhood).

C.3.2 Auxiliary Tasks and Schema Learning

Each schema can be viewed as solving an auxiliary task (Jaderberg et al., 2017) that provides useful gradients for the main RL objective:

Schema Auxiliary Task Learning Signal
\(s_{\text{body}}\) Predict proprioceptive next-state Body movement prediction error
\(s_{\text{spatial}}\) Predict visual-spatial next-state Navigation prediction error
\(s_{\text{aff-hom}}\) Predict homeostatic next-state Interoceptive prediction error
\(s_{\text{attn}}\) Predict which inputs matter Salience prediction error
\(s_{\text{self}}\) Predict social consequences of own actions Agency prediction error

Each auxiliary task provides its own prediction error signal, enabling the system to learn rich representations even when the primary reward signal \(r(t)\) is sparse. This is structurally identical to the use of auxiliary tasks in deep RL to accelerate representation learning.

C.3.3 State Abstraction

The integration step \(\text{Integrate}(\cdot)\) performs state abstraction — compressing the high-dimensional sensory input into a compact representation sufficient for prediction and action selection. The integrated schema vector \(\mathbf{z}(t)\) satisfies two properties:

  1. Sufficiency: \(\mathbf{z}(t)\) retains enough information to predict future rewards: \(r(t+\tau) \perp \mathbf{y}(t) \mid \mathbf{z}(t)\) for all \(\tau > 0\)
  2. Minimality: \(\mathbf{z}(t)\) discards information irrelevant to reward prediction, implementing the information bottleneck principle (Tishby et al., 2000)

The attention schema \(s_{\text{attn}}\) determines what information passes through the bottleneck — implementing the RSP model's claim that attention determines the contents of consciousness.

C.4 Level 3 as Model-Based Planning

C.4.1 Model-Based vs. Model-Free

Standard RL distinguishes:

  • Model-free: learn value/policy directly from experience (habitual behavior)
  • Model-based: learn a world model and plan by simulating future trajectories (deliberate behavior)

The RSP hierarchy implements both:

C.4.2 Planning via Recursive Simulation

RL Type RSP Level Mechanism
Model-free Level 1 → Level 2 Cached affect-to-action mappings (habits, conditioned responses)
Model-based Level 3 Recursive simulation of social consequences
Hybrid Cross-level Model-based predictions guide model-free learning

Level 3 planning proceeds by iteratively simulating:

Step 1. Predict the consequences of a candidate action \(\mathbf{u}_{\text{cand}}\) on the schema state:

$$\hat{\mathbf{z}}(t+1 \mid \mathbf{u}_{\text{cand}}) = g_3^{\text{dyn}}(\mathbf{z}(t), \mathbf{u}_{\text{cand}}, \boldsymbol{\mu}_3^{\text{rec}}(t))$$

Step 2. Predict the reward (valence) of the resulting state:

$$\hat{r}(t+1 \mid \mathbf{u}_{\text{cand}}) = \mathcal{R}(\hat{\mathbf{z}}(t+1)) = -\frac{1}{2}\hat{\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}}_1^T \boldsymbol{\Pi}_1 \hat{\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}}_1$$

Step 3. Predict others' responses to the action (the social model):

$$\hat{o}_j(t+1 \mid \mathbf{u}_{\text{cand}}) = f_o^{\text{pred}}(o_j(t), \mathbf{u}_{\text{cand}})$$

Step 4. Predict the recursive self-model update given others' predicted responses:

$$\hat{\mathcal{M}}^{(d)}(t+1 \mid \mathbf{u}_{\text{cand}}) = \text{RecurseUpdate}\big(\hat{\mathcal{M}}^{(d-1)}, \{\hat{o}_j(t+1)\}\big)$$

Step 5. Evaluate expected free energy of the candidate action:

$$G(\mathbf{u}_{\text{cand}}) = \hat{F}_1 + \hat{F}_2 + \hat{F}_3 + \hat{F}_3^{\text{rec}}$$

Step 6. Select the action minimizing expected free energy:

$$\mathbf{u}^*(t) = \arg\min_{\mathbf{u}_{\text{cand}}} G(\mathbf{u}_{\text{cand}})$$

This planning process is the computational implementation of "thinking before acting" — and specifically, "thinking about what others will think of me if I act this way."

C.4.3 The Social Reward Shaping

The recursive self-model introduces a social component to the reward function that goes beyond individual homeostasis:

$$r_{\text{social}}(t) = -\sum_{d=1}^{D_{\max}} \gamma^d \cdot \frac{1}{2}\big\|\mathcal{M}^{(d)}(t) - \mathcal{M}^{(d-1)}(t)\big\|_{\boldsymbol{\Pi}_3^{(d)}}^2$$

This is a form of reward shaping (Ng et al., 1999): the base reward \(r(t) = v(t)\) is augmented by a social consistency term that penalizes discrepancies between self-model and socially-reflected self-model. The shaped reward:

$$r_{\text{total}}(t) = r(t) + \lambda_{\text{social}} \cdot r_{\text{social}}(t)$$

ensures that the agent values not only homeostatic comfort but also social coherence — explaining why humans will endure physical discomfort (fasting, cold exposure, painful rituals) to maintain social standing.

C.5 The Intrinsically Motivated Learning Pipeline

The model-based planning of §C.4 depends on having a learned world model and a structured state representation. But how are these acquired? The answer is an unsupervised learning pipeline in which each level's outputs bootstrap the next level's learning — a process that must occur in a specific developmental order.

C.5.1 Three Phases of Learning

The RSP model's learning can be understood as three nested RL problems, each operating on a different timescale:

Phase 1: Reward Function Learning (Level 1)

$$\hat{r}(t) = \phi_v\big(\boldsymbol{\Pi}_1(t) \cdot [\mathbf{i}(t) - g_1(\boldsymbol{\mu}_2(t))]\big)$$

The system learns what counts as rewarding by learning to predict its own interoceptive states. This is analogous to intrinsic motivation in RL (Schmidhuber, 1991; Oudeyer & Kaplan, 2007): the reward signal is generated internally through prediction error, not externally specified.

Timescale: Fast (milliseconds). Updated with each new interoceptive observation.

Phase 2: State Representation Learning (Level 2)

$$\mathbf{z}(t) = \text{Integrate}\big(\mathbf{a}(t), \{f_k^{\text{schema}}(\cdot)\}_{k \in \mathcal{S}}\big)$$

The schemas learn to extract the information from sensory input that is relevant for predicting future reward. This is unsupervised representation learning — the schemas are shaped by the statistics of the sensory input and the structure of the reward landscape.

Timescale: Moderate (seconds to minutes for updates; months to years for structural development).

Phase 3: World Model and Policy Learning (Level 3)

$$\pi^*(\mathbf{u} \mid \mathbf{z}, \boldsymbol{\mu}_3^{\text{rec}}) = \arg\min_{\pi} \; G(\pi)$$

The system learns a transition model, a social model, and a policy — all by minimizing expected free energy. The world model includes not only physical dynamics but social dynamics (how others respond to one's actions), making this a multi-agent RL problem solved from the perspective of a single agent with theory of mind.

Timescale: Slow (minutes to hours for tactical planning; years for strategic self-model revision).

C.5.2 Why "Unsupervised"

The term "unsupervised" is justified on three grounds:

  1. No external reward specification. The reward function \(r(t) = v(t)\) is derived from the agent's own interoceptive prediction errors, not from an external signal.
  2. No external state labels. The schemas \(\{s_k\}\) are learned representations, not pre-specified features. The agent discovers which features of its sensory input are relevant for prediction and control.
  3. No external curriculum. The developmental sequence (body schema → spatial → homeostatic → attentional → self-other) emerges from the structure of the learning problem itself — simpler predictions must be stable before more complex predictions can be learned atop them.

C.5.3 Comparison with Deep RL Architectures

C.6 Temporal Difference Learning Across Levels

C.6.1 Level-Specific TD Errors

Feature Standard Deep RL RSP RL
Reward External, fixed Internal, learned (interoceptive surprise)
State Raw pixels or hand-crafted Learned schemas (5 auxiliary tasks)
Model Optional (model-based variants) Required (3-level generative hierarchy)
Multi-agent Separate module Integrated via recursive self-model
Exploration ε-greedy, curiosity bonus Epistemic foraging via expected free energy
Value function Scalar expected return Expected free energy (multi-objective)
Hierarchy Optional (options, feudal) Required (3 levels, each with distinct timescale)

Each level of the hierarchy generates its own temporal difference error:

Level 1 TD (Affective TD):

$$\delta_1(t) = r(t+1) + \gamma_{\text{RL}} \cdot V_1(\mathbf{a}(t+1)) - V_1(\mathbf{a}(t))$$

Tracks whether affect is better or worse than expected. Positive \(\delta_1\) = pleasant surprise. Negative \(\delta_1\) = disappointment. Neural correlate: phasic dopamine in VTA.

Level 2 TD (Schema TD):

$$\delta_2(t) = r(t+1) + \gamma_{\text{RL}} \cdot V_2(\mathbf{z}(t+1)) - V_2(\mathbf{z}(t))$$

Tracks whether the structured situation is better or worse than predicted by the schema integration. Positive \(\delta_2\) = "things are going well." Negative \(\delta_2\) = "this situation is deteriorating." Neural correlate: dopaminergic projections to dorsal striatum.

Level 3 TD (Social TD):

$$\delta_3(t) = r_{\text{total}}(t+1) + \gamma_{\text{RL}} \cdot V_3(\boldsymbol{\mu}_3^{\text{rec}}(t+1)) - V_3(\boldsymbol{\mu}_3^{\text{rec}}(t))$$

Tracks whether the self-model's social prediction was confirmed or violated. Positive \(\delta_3\) = "others see me as I see myself" (social validation). Negative \(\delta_3\) = "I misjudged how others see me" (social surprise, embarrassment). Neural correlate: dopaminergic projections to mPFC, social reward processing.

C.6.2 Cross-Level TD Propagation

TD errors propagate across levels, creating a cascade:

$$\delta_1 \xrightarrow{\text{drives}} \text{schema update} \xrightarrow{\text{generates}} \delta_2 \xrightarrow{\text{drives}} \text{self-model update} \xrightarrow{\text{generates}} \delta_3$$

A sudden loud noise:

  1. Level 1: \(\delta_1 < 0\) (interoceptive surprise, negative valence)
  2. Level 2: \(\delta_2 < 0\) (spatial schema violated — something unexpected in environment)
  3. Level 3: \(\delta_3 \approx 0\) (self-model unaffected, unless in social context where startle response is embarrassing, then \(\delta_3 < 0\))

This cascade formalizes how a simple sensory event can escalate into a social-emotional experience through the hierarchy.

C.7 Exploration, Curiosity, and the Epistemic Drive

C.7.1 Intrinsic Motivation

The expected free energy (Appendix B, §B.7.1) naturally decomposes into pragmatic value (reward-seeking) and epistemic value (uncertainty-reducing):

$$G(\pi) = \underbrace{G_{\text{pragmatic}}(\pi)}_{\text{seek reward}} + \underbrace{G_{\text{epistemic}}(\pi)}_{\text{reduce uncertainty}}$$

The epistemic term provides an intrinsic motivation signal — curiosity — that drives the organism to explore states that resolve uncertainty about its generative model. At Level 3, this becomes specifically social curiosity:

$$G_{\text{epistemic}}^{\text{social}}(\pi) = \mathbb{E}\bigg[\sum_{j=1}^{|\mathcal{O}|} D_{\text{KL}}\big[q(o_j(t+\tau) \mid \mathbf{y}_{t+\tau}) \| q(o_j(t))\big] \;\bigg|\; \pi\bigg]$$

This is the expected information gain about other agents' mental states — the computational drive to understand others that manifests as social curiosity, gossip, and perspective-taking.

C.7.2 Exploration-Exploitation in Social Contexts

The exploration-exploitation trade-off takes a distinctive form at Level 3:

  • Exploitation: Act in ways that maintain the current self-model and social relationships (conformity, consistency)
  • Exploration: Act in novel ways that could update the self-model and reveal new social information (risk-taking, self-disclosure, challenging social norms)

The balance is governed by the precision of the self-model \(\boldsymbol{\Pi}_3\): high self-model precision favors exploitation (rigid self-concept), low precision favors exploration (flexible self-concept). This connects to personality psychology: trait openness corresponds to low \(\boldsymbol{\Pi}_3\), while trait conscientiousness corresponds to high \(\boldsymbol{\Pi}_3\).

C.8 Summary: The RL Architecture of Consciousness

The RSP model implements a complete reinforcement learning system:

Level Role Key Equations TD Error
LEVEL 3: Model-Based Planner Plans by simulating social world and self World model: \(g_3(\text{social dynamics, self-model})\) \(\delta_3\) (social validation / surprise)
Policy: \(\pi^*(u \mid z, \mathcal{M}^{\text{rec}}) = \arg\min G(u)\)
Social reward: $$r_{\text{social}} = -\frac{1}{2}\ \mathcal{M}^{(d)} - \mathcal{M}^{(d-1)}\ _{\boldsymbol{\Pi}_3^{(d)}}^2$$
LEVEL 2: State Representation Learner Learns structured state representations Schemas: \(\{s_{\text{body}}, s_{\text{spatial}}, s_{\text{aff-hom}}, s_{\text{attn}}, s_{\text{self}}\}\) \(\delta_2\) (situation improving / deteriorating)
Integration: \(z(t) = \text{Integrate}(a(t), \{s_k(t)\})\)
Auxiliary tasks: 5 prediction errors \(\to\) gradients
LEVEL 1: Reward Function Generator Generates intrinsic reward from interoception Reward: \(r(t) = v(t) = \varphi_v(\Pi_1 \cdot \varepsilon_1)\) \(\delta_1\) (pleasant / unpleasant surprise)
Interoceptive prediction: \(g_1(\mu_2)\)
ENVIRONMENT Body + physical world + social world Sensory input: \(i(t) \uparrow\) / Motor output: \(u(t) \downarrow\)

Signal flow: Environment \(\xrightarrow{i(t)}\) Level 1 \(\xrightarrow{a(t)}\) Level 2 \(\xrightarrow{z(t)}\) Level 3 (ascending). Level 3 \(\xrightarrow{\text{predictions}}\) Level 2 \(\xrightarrow{\text{predictions}}\) Level 1 \(\xrightarrow{u(t)}\) Environment (descending).

Consciousness emerges when all three RL levels operate together: the system generates its own rewards (Level 1), learns its own state representation (Level 2), and plans by recursively simulating its social world (Level 3). The "what it is like" of experience is the felt quality of this multi-level optimization — the sense of being an agent that cares about outcomes (valence), understands its situation (schemas), and knows itself through others' eyes (recursive self-model).

References for Appendix C

  • Critchley, H. D., & Harrison, N. A. (2013). Visceral influences on brain and behavior. Neuron, 77(4), 624-638.
  • Jaderberg, M., et al. (2017). Reinforcement learning with unsupervised auxiliary tasks. ICLR 2017.
  • Ng, A. Y., Harada, D., & Russell, S. (1999). Policy invariance under reward transformations. ICML 1999, 278-287.
  • Oudeyer, P.-Y., & Kaplan, F. (2007). What is intrinsic motivation? A typology of computational approaches. Frontiers in Neurorobotics, 1, 6.
  • Schmidhuber, J. (1991). Curious model-building control systems. IEEE International Joint Conference on Neural Networks, 1458-1463.
  • Schultz, W. (1998). Predictive reward signal of dopamine neurons. Journal of Neurophysiology, 80(1), 1-27.
  • Sutton, R. S., & Barto, A. G. (2018). Reinforcement Learning: An Introduction (2nd ed.). MIT Press.
  • Tishby, N., Pereira, F. C., & Bialek, W. (2000). The information bottleneck method. Proceedings of the 37th Allerton Conference, 368-377.

Appendix D: Control-Theoretic Dynamics of the Strange Loop

Overview

This appendix models the Recursive Social Prediction (RSP) hierarchy as a multi-loop feedback control system. We specify reference signals, comparators, gains, and transfer functions at each level, analyze the stability conditions for the strange loop, and show how control-theoretic concepts (set-points, integral control, cascade control, stability margins) illuminate the dynamics of consciousness, emotion regulation, and psychopathology.

The formulation draws on Perceptual Control Theory (Powers, 1973, 2005), modern control theory (Åström & Murray, 2021), and the hierarchical control perspective in cognitive science (Carver & Scheier, 1998).

Notation. All symbols from Appendix A are retained. New control-theoretic notation is introduced in §D.1.

D.1 Control-Theoretic Notation

D.1.1 New Symbols

D.1.2 RSP-to-Control Mapping

D.2 The RSP as a Cascade Control System

D.2.1 Cascade Architecture

Symbol Domain Definition
\(\mathbf{r}_l(t)\) \(\mathbb{R}^{n_l}\) Reference signal (set-point) at level \(l\)
\(\mathbf{p}_l(t)\) \(\mathbb{R}^{n_l}\) Perceptual signal (measured output) at level \(l\)
\(\mathbf{e}_l^{\text{ctrl}}(t)\) \(\mathbb{R}^{n_l}\) Control error: \(\mathbf{r}_l - \mathbf{p}_l\)
\(K_l\) \(\mathbb{R}^{n_l \times n_l}\) Proportional gain matrix at level \(l\)
\(K_l^I\) \(\mathbb{R}^{n_l \times n_l}\) Integral gain matrix at level \(l\)
\(K_l^D\) \(\mathbb{R}^{n_l \times n_l}\) Derivative gain matrix at level \(l\)
\(G_l(s)\) \(\mathbb{C}^{n_l \times n_l}\) Transfer function of plant (controlled process) at level \(l\)
\(C_l(s)\) \(\mathbb{C}^{n_l \times n_l}\) Transfer function of controller at level \(l\)
\(L_l(s)\) \(\mathbb{C}^{n_l \times n_l}\) Loop transfer function: \(L_l = C_l \cdot G_l\)
\(\lambda_{\max}(L_l)\) \(\mathbb{C}\) Maximum eigenvalue of \(L_l\) (determines stability)
\(\phi_m\) \(\mathbb{R}\) Phase margin (degrees)
\(g_m\) \(\mathbb{R}\) Gain margin (dB)
\(\tau_l\) \(\mathbb{R}_{>0}\) Time constant of level \(l\) dynamics
Control Concept RSP Equivalent
Reference signal \(\mathbf{r}_l\) Top-down prediction from level \(l+1\): \(g_{l+1}(\boldsymbol{\mu}_{l+1})\)
Perceptual signal \(\mathbf{p}_l\) Bottom-up input: actual state at level \(l\)
Control error \(\mathbf{e}_l^{\text{ctrl}}\) Prediction error \(\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_l\) (with sign reversed)
Controller output Expectation update \(\dot{\boldsymbol{\mu}}_l\) (perception) or action \(\mathbf{u}\)
Plant Body + environment + social world
Set-point Homeostatic target (Level 1), schema expectation (Level 2), self-concept (Level 3)
Cascade control Three-level hierarchy with nested feedback loops

Cascade control uses nested feedback loops: an outer (slow) loop sets the reference for a middle loop, which sets the reference for an inner (fast) loop. The RSP hierarchy is a three-level cascade:

Level Reference Sensor Error Output Time Constant
Level 3: Outer Loop Self-concept \(r_3\) (who I am) Social feedback \(\to p_3\) \(e_3 = r_3 - p_3\) (self-model discrepancy) Schema set-points \(r_2\) \(\tau_3 \sim\) minutes--hours
Level 2: Middle Loop Schema targets \(r_2\) (expected situation) Multimodal sensory \(\to p_2\) \(e_2 = r_2 - p_2\) (schema mismatch) Interoceptive set-points \(r_1\) \(\tau_2 \sim\) seconds--minutes
Level 1: Inner Loop Homeostatic targets \(r_1\) (body state) Interoceptive signals \(\to p_1\) \(e_1 = r_1 - p_1\) (homeostatic deviation) Autonomic/motor action \(u(t)\) \(\tau_1 \sim\) milliseconds--seconds
Plant Body + environment + social world

Signal flow: Level 3 \(\xrightarrow{r_2}\) Level 2 \(\xrightarrow{r_1}\) Level 1 \(\xrightarrow{u(t)}\) Plant (descending references). Plant \(\xrightarrow{i(t)}\) Level 1 \(\xrightarrow{p_2}\) Level 2 \(\xrightarrow{p_3}\) Level 3 (ascending feedback).

D.2.2 Level-Specific Control Laws

Level 1: Homeostatic Controller (PID)

The inner loop implements proportional-integral-derivative (PID) control on body state:

$$\mathbf{u}(t) = K_1 \cdot \mathbf{e}_1^{\text{ctrl}}(t) + K_1^I \int_0^t \mathbf{e}_1^{\text{ctrl}}(\tau) \, d\tau + K_1^D \cdot \dot{\mathbf{e}}_1^{\text{ctrl}}(t)$$

where:

  • \(\mathbf{e}_1^{\text{ctrl}}(t) = \mathbf{r}_1(t) - \mathbf{i}(t)\) is the homeostatic error
  • \(K_1\) is the proportional gain (immediate response to deviation)
  • \(K_1^I\) is the integral gain (eliminates steady-state error — the body doesn't tolerate persistent homeostatic deviation)
  • \(K_1^D\) is the derivative gain (anticipatory response to rate of change)

The integral term is critical: it ensures zero steady-state error for homeostatic variables (temperature, blood glucose, pH). Biologically, this maps to the hypothalamic set-point mechanisms.

Level 2: Schema Controller (Proportional with Adaptive Gain)

$$\dot{\boldsymbol{\mu}}_2(t) = K_2(t) \cdot \mathbf{e}_2^{\text{ctrl}}(t) + K_2^D \cdot \dot{\mathbf{e}}_2^{\text{ctrl}}(t)$$

where:

  • \(\mathbf{e}_2^{\text{ctrl}}(t) = g_3(\boldsymbol{\mu}_3(t)) - \boldsymbol{\mu}_1(t)\) is the schema mismatch
  • \(K_2(t) = \boldsymbol{\Pi}_2(t)\) — the gain is the precision matrix, making this an adaptive controller whose gain changes with confidence

The attention schema \(s_{\text{attn}}\) modulates \(K_2\) for each schema channel, implementing gain scheduling: different schemas receive different control authority depending on the attentional state.

Level 3: Self-Model Controller (Predictive with Recursion)

$$\dot{\boldsymbol{\mu}}_3(t) = K_3 \cdot \mathbf{e}_3^{\text{ctrl}}(t) + K_3^{\text{pred}} \cdot \hat{\mathbf{e}}_3^{\text{ctrl}}(t+1)$$

where:

  • \(\mathbf{e}_3^{\text{ctrl}}(t) = \mathcal{M}^{(0)}(t) - \mathcal{M}^{(D_{\max})}(t)\) is the discrepancy between direct self-model and socially-reflected self-model
  • \(\hat{\mathbf{e}}_3^{\text{ctrl}}(t+1)\) is the predicted future self-model error (model predictive control)
  • \(K_3^{\text{pred}}\) is the predictive gain

This is Model Predictive Control (MPC): the controller uses a model of future dynamics to optimize actions over a planning horizon.

D.3 Transfer Functions and Frequency Analysis

D.3.1 Linearized Transfer Functions

Linearizing around the operating point \((\boldsymbol{\mu}_l^*, \mathbf{r}_l^*)\), each level has a transfer function in the Laplace domain (\(s = j\omega\)):

Level 1 Plant:

$$G_1(s) = \frac{\mathbf{P}_1(s)}{\mathbf{U}(s)} = \frac{A_1}{s + \frac{1}{\tau_1}}$$

where \(A_1\) is the plant gain (sensitivity of interoceptive state to motor commands) and \(\tau_1\) is the body's time constant (e.g., ~2 seconds for heart rate response).

Level 1 Controller (PID):

$$C_1(s) = K_1 + \frac{K_1^I}{s} + K_1^D \cdot s$$

Level 1 Loop Transfer Function:

$$L_1(s) = C_1(s) \cdot G_1(s) = \frac{(K_1 s + K_1^I + K_1^D s^2) \cdot A_1}{s(s + \frac{1}{\tau_1})}$$

The closed-loop transfer function for each level \(l\):

$$T_l(s) = \frac{L_l(s)}{1 + L_l(s)}$$

D.3.2 Timescale Separation

For the cascade to be stable, the inner loop must be faster than the outer loop. The timescale separation condition:

$$\tau_1 \ll \tau_2 \ll \tau_3$$

Typical biological values:

Level Time Constant \(\tau_l\) Bandwidth \(\omega_l = 1/\tau_l\) Biological Process
\(l = 1\) \(\tau_1 \approx 0.5\text{–}2\) s \(0.5\text{–}2\) Hz Autonomic reflexes, heart rate, respiration
\(l = 2\) \(\tau_2 \approx 5\text{–}30\) s \(0.03\text{–}0.2\) Hz Schema updates, emotional episodes, reorientation
\(l = 3\) \(\tau_3 \approx 60\text{–}3600\) s \(0.0003\text{–}0.017\) Hz Self-model revision, social recalibration

The ratio \(\tau_{l+1}/\tau_l \geq 5\) ensures that each level's controller "sees" the inner loop as essentially instantaneous — a standard cascade design principle.

D.4 Stability Analysis

D.4.1 Local Stability: Eigenvalue Analysis

The linearized dynamics of the full three-level system:

$$\dot{\boldsymbol{\mu}} = \mathbf{A} \cdot \boldsymbol{\mu} + \mathbf{B} \cdot \mathbf{r}$$

where \(\boldsymbol{\mu} = [\boldsymbol{\mu}_1; \boldsymbol{\mu}_2; \boldsymbol{\mu}_3]\) is the stacked state vector and \(\mathbf{A}\) is the Jacobian of the coupled dynamics:

$$\mathbf{A} = \begin{pmatrix} -K_1 G_1' & J_{12} & 0 \\ J_{21} & -K_2 G_2' & J_{23} \\ 0 & J_{32} & -K_3 G_3' + J_{33}^{\text{rec}} \end{pmatrix}$$

where:

  • Diagonal blocks \(-K_l G_l'\) are the local feedback terms (stabilizing when negative)
  • Off-diagonal blocks \(J_{lm}\) capture inter-level coupling
  • \(J_{33}^{\text{rec}}\) is the self-referential Jacobian from the recursive loop

Stability condition: All eigenvalues of \(\mathbf{A}\) must have negative real parts:

$$\text{Re}(\lambda_i(\mathbf{A})) < 0 \quad \forall i$$

D.4.2 The Strange Loop Stability Condition

The recursive self-model at Level 3 creates a positive feedback path (self-referential loop). For stability, this positive feedback must be dominated by the negative feedback from prediction error correction:

$$\rho(J_{33}^{\text{rec}}) < \sigma_{\min}\!\left(K_3 \cdot \frac{\partial g_3}{\partial \boldsymbol{\mu}_3^{\text{rec}}}\right)$$

where \(\rho(\cdot)\) is the spectral radius and \(\sigma_{\min}(\cdot)\) is the smallest singular value. In plain terms: the rate at which the self-model amplifies itself through recursive social reflection must be less than the rate at which prediction errors correct the self-model.

Convergence of the strange loop. The recursion \(\mathcal{M}^{(d)} = (1-\beta_d)\mathcal{M}^{(d-1)} + \beta_d \hat{\mathcal{M}}^{(d)}\) converges if:

$$\beta_d = \beta_0 \gamma^d \quad \text{with} \quad \beta_0 < 1, \; \gamma < 1$$

The total gain of the recursive path is:

$$G_{\text{rec}} = \sum_{d=1}^{D_{\max}} \beta_0 \gamma^d = \beta_0 \cdot \frac{\gamma(1 - \gamma^{D_{\max}})}{1 - \gamma}$$

For \(\beta_0 = 0.3\), \(\gamma = 0.5\), \(D_{\max} = 5\): \(G_{\text{rec}} \approx 0.29\) — well below 1, ensuring stable convergence.

D.4.3 Phase and Gain Margins

For each loop, the phase margin \(\phi_m\) and gain margin \(g_m\) quantify robustness:

$$\phi_m = 180° + \angle L_l(j\omega_c)$$

where \(\omega_c\) is the gain crossover frequency (\(|L_l(j\omega_c)| = 1\)).

Estimated minimum margins for stable consciousness (order-of-magnitude values based on engineering analogy with cascade control systems; empirical neural measurements would refine these):

Level Minimum \(\phi_m\) Minimum \(g_m\) Interpretation
\(l = 1\) \(> 45°\) \(> 6\) dB Stable autonomic regulation without oscillation
\(l = 2\) \(> 30°\) \(> 4\) dB Schema updates converge without rumination
\(l = 3\) \(> 20°\) \(> 3\) dB Self-model stable under social perturbation

The margins decrease at higher levels because the plant becomes less predictable (social dynamics are more uncertain than autonomic dynamics), and the system tolerates more oscillation (mild self-doubt is normal; mild body temperature oscillation is not). These values follow standard cascade design practice for fast/moderate/slow loops (Skogestad & Postlethwaite, 2005) and should be interpreted as predictions to be tested against neural data, not as established empirical parameters.

D.5 Disturbance Rejection and Emotion Regulation

D.5.1 Disturbance Model

External disturbances enter at each level:

$$\mathbf{p}_l(t) = G_l(s) \cdot \mathbf{u}_{l}(t) + \mathbf{d}_l(t)$$

where \(\mathbf{d}_l\) is the disturbance:

  • \(\mathbf{d}_1\): unexpected interoceptive events (pain, illness, drug effects)
  • \(\mathbf{d}_2\): unexpected environmental changes (threats, surprises, novelty)
  • \(\mathbf{d}_3\): unexpected social events (rejection, praise, betrayal)

D.5.2 Sensitivity Functions

The sensitivity function \(S_l(s)\) determines how well level \(l\) rejects disturbances:

$$S_l(s) = \frac{1}{1 + L_l(s)}$$
  • \(|S_l(j\omega)| < 1\): disturbances at frequency \(\omega\) are attenuated (effective regulation)
  • \(|S_l(j\omega)| > 1\): disturbances at frequency \(\omega\) are amplified (regulation failure)

The complementary sensitivity \(T_l(s) = 1 - S_l(s)\) determines reference tracking. The fundamental constraint:

$$S_l(s) + T_l(s) = 1$$

This means perfect disturbance rejection and perfect reference tracking cannot coexist — there is always a trade-off. In emotional terms: you cannot simultaneously be perfectly responsive to new emotional information (low \(S\)) and perfectly stable in your self-concept (high \(T\)). This is the control-theoretic formulation of the flexibility-stability trade-off in emotion regulation.

D.5.3 Emotion Regulation Strategies as Control Reconfigurations

Different emotion regulation strategies correspond to different modifications of the control architecture:

Strategy Control Operation Mathematical Effect
Reappraisal Change \(\mathbf{r}_2\) (schema reference) New set-point: \(\mathbf{r}_2' = f_{\text{reappraise}}(\mathbf{r}_2, \text{context})\)
Suppression Reduce \(K_1\) (gain reduction) Attenuated autonomic response: \(K_1' = \alpha K_1\), \(\alpha < 1\)
Attention deployment Modify \(\boldsymbol{\Pi}_2\) (precision/gain) Selective gain: \(K_{2,k}' \to 0\) for distressing schema \(k\)
Situation selection Change plant input (avoidance) Eliminate disturbance: \(\mathbf{d}_l = 0\) by removing source
Acceptance Increase \(S_l\) bandwidth Allow disturbance passage without amplification
Rumination Positive feedback in Level 3 loop \(G_{\text{rec}} \to 1\) (marginal stability, oscillation)

Reappraisal is the most powerful strategy because it operates at Level 2–3, changing the reference signals that the lower loops track. This aligns with empirical evidence that cognitive reappraisal is more effective than suppression for long-term emotion regulation (Gross, 2002).

D.6 Pathological Dynamics

D.6.1 Instability Modes

Control systems fail in characteristic ways. Each failure mode maps to a recognizable psychopathological pattern:

Limit cycle oscillation (\(\phi_m \to 0\)):

When the phase margin at Level 3 approaches zero, the self-model oscillates between states without converging:

$$\mathcal{M}(t) \approx \mathcal{M}^* + A \sin(\omega_{\text{osc}} t)$$

Clinical analog: Bipolar disorder — the self-model oscillates between grandiosity (\(\mathcal{M}\) overestimates social standing) and depression (\(\mathcal{M}\) underestimates). The oscillation frequency \(\omega_{\text{osc}}\) corresponds to the cycle period (weeks to months).

Integrator windup (\(K_1^I\) accumulates during saturation):

When the Level 1 controller's integral term accumulates error during prolonged disturbance that exceeds the actuator's capacity:

$$\int_0^T \mathbf{e}_1^{\text{ctrl}}(\tau) d\tau \to \infty \quad \text{while} \quad \mathbf{u}_{\max} \text{ is saturated}$$

Clinical analog: Chronic stress — the body's homeostatic controller accumulates error (allostatic load) when the stressor exceeds the capacity for autonomic correction. When the stressor is removed, the accumulated integral term causes overshoot (burnout, immune dysfunction).

Positive feedback runaway (\(G_{\text{rec}} \geq 1\)):

When the recursive self-model gain exceeds unity, the strange loop amplifies rather than converges:

$$\|\mathcal{M}^{(d)} - \mathcal{M}^{(d-1)}\| > \|\mathcal{M}^{(d-1)} - \mathcal{M}^{(d-2)}\| \quad \forall d$$

Clinical analog: Paranoid ideation — "they think I think they think..." escalates rather than settling, because each recursion amplifies the social threat signal.

Loss of reference signal (\(\mathbf{r}_3 \to \mathbf{0}\)):

When the Level 3 reference signal (self-concept) degrades:

$$\|\mathbf{r}_3\| \to 0 \implies \mathbf{e}_3^{\text{ctrl}} \to -\mathbf{p}_3$$

Clinical analog: Depersonalization/derealization — without a clear self-concept, the controller has no target; perception continues but lacks the self-referential structure that gives experience its "mine-ness."

Sensor failure (\(\mathbf{p}_l \to \text{noise}\)):

When the perceptual signal is dominated by noise rather than veridical information:

$$\text{SNR}_l = \frac{\|\mathbf{p}_l^{\text{signal}}\|}{\|\mathbf{p}_l^{\text{noise}}\|} \to 0$$

Clinical analog: Psychosis — sensory signals (Level 1–2) or social signals (Level 3) become unreliable, and the controller acts on hallucinated or delusional input.

D.6.2 Robustness and Resilience

Robustness is the system's ability to maintain performance under parameter variation (gain changes, time constant shifts). Formally:

$$\text{Robustness} = \min_l \min\left(\frac{\phi_{m,l}}{\phi_{m,l}^{\min}}, \frac{g_{m,l}}{g_{m,l}^{\min}}\right)$$

Resilience is the system's ability to return to stable operation after a large disturbance. This depends on the region of attraction — the set of initial conditions from which the nonlinear system converges to the desired operating point.

The RSP model predicts that resilience is enhanced by:

  1. Wide timescale separation (\(\tau_3/\tau_1 \gg 1\)): prevents fast disturbances from destabilizing slow loops
  2. Moderate recursive gain (\(G_{\text{rec}} \approx 0.3\)): enough self-awareness without self-amplification
  3. Flexible precision (\(\boldsymbol{\Pi}_l\) adaptive): attention can reallocate to compensate for disturbances
  4. Rich schema repertoire (high \(|\mathcal{C}|\)): more conceptual categories = more reappraisal options = more reference signals to choose from

D.7 The Strange Loop as a Fixed-Point Iteration

The pathological dynamics of §D.6 showed what happens when the control system fails. We now provide the formal tool for understanding why the strange loop is normally stable — the contraction mapping theorem — and what it means precisely for the recursive self-model to converge.

D.7.1 Fixed-Point Formulation

The recursive self-model can be formulated as a fixed-point problem. Define the mapping:

$$\Psi: \mathcal{M} \mapsto (1 - \beta) \cdot \mathcal{M} + \beta \cdot \text{SocialReflect}(\mathcal{M}, \{o_j\})$$

where \(\text{SocialReflect}(\mathcal{M}, \{o_j\})\) computes the average predicted self-as-seen-by-others. The fixed point \(\mathcal{M}^*\) satisfies:

$$\mathcal{M}^* = \Psi(\mathcal{M}^*)$$

D.7.2 Contraction Mapping Theorem

\(\Psi\) is a contraction mapping if there exists \(\rho \in [0,1)\) such that:

$$\|\Psi(\mathcal{M}_a) - \Psi(\mathcal{M}_b)\| \leq \rho \cdot \|\mathcal{M}_a - \mathcal{M}_b\| \quad \forall \mathcal{M}_a, \mathcal{M}_b$$

The contraction coefficient:

$$\rho = (1 - \beta) + \beta \cdot L, \quad \text{where } L = \left\|\frac{1}{|\mathcal{O}|}\sum_j w_j \cdot \frac{\partial \hat{\mathcal{M}}_j}{\partial \mathcal{M}}\right\$$

The fixed point exists and is unique when \(\rho < 1\), which requires \(L < 1\) — the social reflection mapping must be contractive (averaging over multiple social perspectives dampens extremes). The convergence rate is geometric:

$$\|\mathcal{M}^{(d)} - \mathcal{M}^*\| \leq \rho^d \cdot \|\mathcal{M}^{(0)} - \mathcal{M}^*\$$

For \(\rho = 0.5\) and convergence threshold \(\delta_{\text{conv}} = 0.01\), convergence requires \(d \geq \lceil \log(0.01) / \log(0.5) \rceil \approx 7\) iterations — consistent with the \(D_{\max} \approx 3\text{–}5\) observed empirically (some iterations contribute sub-threshold updates).

D.7.3 Multiple Fixed Points

In the nonlinear regime, \(\Psi\) may have multiple fixed points — stable self-concepts that the system can converge to depending on initial conditions. The basin of attraction for each fixed point determines which self-concept is adopted given the current social context.

Identity switching (e.g., code-switching between cultural identities) corresponds to the system transitioning between basins of attraction under social context changes. The transition occurs when a disturbance pushes \(\mathcal{M}(t)\) across the basin boundary.

D.8 Observer Design and Consciousness

D.8.1 The Self as State Observer

In control theory, an observer (or state estimator) reconstructs the internal state of a plant from its inputs and outputs. The RSP self-model is a state observer for the agent's own cognitive-affective system:

$$\dot{\hat{\boldsymbol{\mu}}} = f(\hat{\boldsymbol{\mu}}, \mathbf{u}) + \mathbf{L} \cdot (\mathbf{y} - \hat{\mathbf{y}})$$

where:

  • \(\hat{\boldsymbol{\mu}}\) is the estimated internal state (self-model)
  • \(\mathbf{L}\) is the observer gain matrix
  • \(\mathbf{y} - \hat{\mathbf{y}}\) is the innovation (prediction error)

This is a Luenberger observer — the same structure as hierarchical predictive coding (Appendix A). The self-model is literally an observer of the self, in the technical control-theoretic sense.

D.8.2 Observability and Consciousness

A system is observable if its internal state can be reconstructed from its outputs. The observability matrix is:

$$\mathcal{O}_{\text{obs}} = \begin{pmatrix} \mathbf{C} \\ \mathbf{C}\mathbf{A} \\ \mathbf{C}\mathbf{A}^2 \\ \vdots \end{pmatrix}$$

where \(\mathbf{C}\) is the output matrix specifying which internal states are accessible to the self-model (i.e., which states the observer can "see"), and \(\mathbf{A}\) is the system dynamics matrix from §D.4.1. The system is fully observable iff \(\text{rank}(\mathcal{O}_{\text{obs}}) = n\) (full state dimension).

In the RSP model, observability is graded rather than binary: the proportion of internal states reconstructable by the self-model varies with the rank of \(\mathbf{O}_{\text{obs}}\) relative to \(n\). The control-theoretic proposal is that conscious access tracks self-observability — the states that reach awareness are precisely those reconstructable by the Level 3 observer. This is a functionalist identification: it equates a mathematical property (observability rank) with a phenomenal property (degree of self-awareness). The identification is motivated by the structural correspondence between the Luenberger observer (§D.8.1) and the hierarchical predictive coding architecture (Appendix A), but it remains a philosophical commitment, not a mathematical derivation.

Full observability = full self-awareness. Partial observability = limited self-awareness (some states are "unconscious" — active but not reconstructed by the observer).

The attention schema \(s_{\text{attn}}\) determines which states are included in the output matrix \(\mathbf{C}\) — implementing the RSP model's claim that attention determines the contents of consciousness.

D.8.3 Separation Principle

The separation principle states that controller design and observer design can be performed independently — the optimal controller for the known-state case remains optimal when paired with an optimal observer.

Applied to RSP: the organism can separately optimize (a) how it acts given what it believes about itself and (b) how it infers its own states from sensory data. This separation is approximate in the nonlinear RSP model, but it explains why self-knowledge and self-regulation can develop somewhat independently — a person can have accurate self-knowledge but poor emotional regulation, or effective regulation strategies based on inaccurate self-models.

D.9 Summary: The Control Architecture of Consciousness

$$\boxed{ \text{Consciousness} = \underbrace{\text{Cascade Control}}_{\text{3-level hierarchy}} + \underbrace{\text{State Observer}}_{\text{self-model}} + \underbrace{\text{Fixed-Point Recursion}}_{\text{strange loop}} }$$

The RSP model, viewed through control theory, is a cascade control system where:

  1. Level 1 implements PID homeostatic control (fast inner loop)
  2. Level 2 implements adaptive gain-scheduled schema control (middle loop)
  3. Level 3 implements model predictive control with recursive state estimation (slow outer loop)
  4. The strange loop is a fixed-point iteration converging to a stable self-concept
  5. Consciousness is the observability of the system to its own state observer
  6. Emotions are the control errors at each level
  7. Attention is gain scheduling across control channels
  8. Psychopathology is control instability (oscillation, windup, runaway, sensor failure)

The control-theoretic perspective offers something the free energy and RL frameworks do not: a precise language for stability, robustness, and failure modes. It predicts not only how consciousness works when it works, but how and why it breaks down — and what parameters must be restored for recovery.

References for Appendix D
