  • Carver, C. S., & Scheier, M. F. (1998). On the Self-Regulation of Behavior. Cambridge University Press.
  • Gross, J. J. (2002). Emotion regulation: Affective, cognitive, and social consequences. Psychophysiology, 39(3), 281-291.
  • Powers, W. T. (1973). Behavior: The Control of Perception. Aldine.
  • Powers, W. T. (2005). Behavior: The Control of Perception (2nd ed.). Benchmark Publications.
  • Skogestad, S., & Postlethwaite, I. (2005). Multivariable Feedback Control: Analysis and Design. Wiley.
  • Stephan, K. E., et al. (2016). Allostatic self-efficacy: A metacognitive theory of dyshomeostasis-induced fatigue and depression. Frontiers in Human Neuroscience, 10, 550.

Appendix E — Unified Mathematical Framework

Overview

Appendixes A–D formalize the RSP model from four distinct mathematical traditions: hierarchical predictive coding, variational free energy, unsupervised reinforcement learning, and cascade feedback control. A natural question arises: are these four separate theories, or four descriptions of one theory?

This appendix demonstrates that they are the latter. We identify a single master variational functional from which all four formalisms are derivable as special cases, prove key equivalences between their central quantities, and extract emergent properties visible only from the unified perspective. The result is a mathematically unified RSP theory in three equations.

E.1 Four Windows on One Architecture

In classical mechanics, the same physical dynamics admit four equivalent mathematical formulations:

  • Newtonian mechanics foregrounds forces and accelerations (\(\mathbf{F} = m\mathbf{a}\)).
  • Lagrangian mechanics foregrounds energy differences and variational principles (\(\delta \int L \, dt = 0\)).
  • Hamiltonian mechanics foregrounds phase space and canonical transformations (\(\dot{q} = \partial H/\partial p\)).
  • Hamilton-Jacobi theory foregrounds wavefronts and optimal control (\(\partial S/\partial t + H = 0\)).

No physicist asks "which is the real mechanics?" The physics lives in their common mathematical core; each formulation reveals different structural features and offers different computational advantages. Physicists discovered this unity over roughly two centuries (Newton 1687, Lagrange 1788, Hamilton 1835, Jacobi 1837).

Appendixes A–D stand in an analogous — though not identical — relationship. The classical mechanics equivalences are mathematically exact; the RSP correspondences are approximate, holding under the Laplace approximation with Gaussian noise near equilibrium. The analogy is aspirational in its precision but genuine in its structure:

Formalism Mechanical Analogue Primary Variable What It Reveals Best
Predictive Coding (A) Newtonian (forces) Prediction error \(\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_l\) Neural message-passing architecture
Free Energy (B) Lagrangian (variational) Free energy \(F\) Global optimality, why three levels
Reinforcement Learning (C) Hamiltonian (state-action) Value \(V\), policy \(\pi\) Learning, planning, development
Control Theory (D) Hamilton-Jacobi (optimal control) Error \(\mathbf{e}_l^{\text{ctrl}}\), gain \(K_l\) Stability, robustness, failure modes

An important disanalogy strengthens rather than weakens the argument: the four mechanical formulations were discovered independently over centuries and their equivalence was a mathematical surprise. The RSP model's four formalisms were developed by drawing on independently established mathematical traditions (Bayesian inference, information theory, reinforcement learning, control engineering), and the fact that they converge on the same dynamics when applied to a three-level recursive hierarchy is evidence that the architecture captures genuine structure — not that the author designed them to agree.

The strategy of this appendix is to: (i) define a unified notation, (ii) identify the master functional, (iii) prove equivalences, (iv) identify divergences, and (v) extract emergent insights.

E.2 Master Variable Correspondence

Table E.1 is the Rosetta Stone of the RSP model, mapping every core variable across all four formalisms. We introduce a unified notation (leftmost column) used throughout this appendix.

Table E.1a: State and Error Variables

Unified Pred. Coding (A) Free Energy (B) RL (C) Control (D) Interpretation
\(\mathbf{x}_l\) \(\boldsymbol{\mu}_l\) (expectations) \(\boldsymbol{\mu}_l\) (sufficient statistics) \(\mathbf{s}_l^{\text{RL}}\) (state) \(\mathbf{p}_l\) (perceptual signal) Internal belief at level \(l\)
\(\mathbf{e}_l\) \(\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_l = \text{input}_l - g_l(\boldsymbol{\mu}_{l+1})\) \(\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_l\) (accuracy term) \(\delta_l\) (TD error; see §E.4) \(\mathbf{e}_l^{\text{ctrl}} = \mathbf{r}_l - \mathbf{p}_l = -\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_l\) Discrepancy driving update
\(\mathbf{W}_l\) \(\boldsymbol{\Pi}_l\) (precision) \(\boldsymbol{\Pi}_l\) (precision in \(F_l\)) exploration-exploitation \(K_l\) (gain); \(K_2 = \boldsymbol{\Pi}_2\) at Level 2 Error weighting / attention
\(\boldsymbol{\eta}_l\) temporal pred. error \(F_{\text{temporal}}\) term related to TD error across time \(\dot{\mathbf{e}}_l^{\text{ctrl}}\) (derivative term) Temporal consistency
\(\mathcal{M}\) self-model state \(\mathcal{M}(t)\) \(\boldsymbol{\mu}_3^{\text{rec}}\) Level 3 planning state state being controlled at Level 3 (not the reference \(r_3\)) Recursive identity

Table E.1b: Dynamics and Convergence Variables

Unified Pred. Coding (A) Free Energy (B) RL (C) Control (D) Interpretation
\(\mathcal{J}\) $$\sum_l \ \boldsymbol{\xi}_l\ ^2$$ \(F = \sum_l F_l\) \(-V\) (negative value; see §E.4) $$\sum_l \ \mathbf{e}_l^{\text{ctrl}}\ _{K_l}^2$$ Objective to minimize
\(g_l(\cdot)\) generative function generative model transition model \(\mathcal{T}\) plant \(G_l(s)\) How level \(l+1\) predicts \(l\)
\(\dot{\mathbf{x}}_l\) \(\dot{\boldsymbol{\mu}}_l\) (message passing) \(-\kappa_l \partial F/\partial \boldsymbol{\mu}_l\) TD update \(K_l \mathbf{e}_l^{\text{ctrl}}\) Dynamics of belief revision
\(\tau_l\) implicit in \(\kappa_l\) \(\kappa_1 \gg \kappa_2 \gg \kappa_3\) three learning phases \(\tau_1 \ll \tau_2 \ll \tau_3\) Timescale separation
\(\mathbf{S}_l\) \(\boldsymbol{\Sigma}_l\) (transition noise) \(\boldsymbol{\Sigma}_l\) (in \(F_{\text{temporal}}\)) — — Temporal noise covariance

Note on the RL column: The mapping between prediction errors (\(\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_l\)) and TD errors (\(\delta_l\)) is not a direct identity but an approximate correspondence that holds at fast timescales (see Proposition E.2). The mapping between \(\boldsymbol{\eta}_l\) (temporal prediction errors) and TD errors is also indirect: both capture temporal mismatch, but TD errors additionally involve the discount factor and value function.

Note on \(\mathcal{M}\): In App D, the self-model \(\mathcal{M}\) is the state being controlled at Level 3, not the reference signal \(r_3\). The reference \(r_3\) represents the desired self-concept (set-point), while \(\mathcal{M}^{(D_{\max})}\) is the current self-model state. The control error \(e_3^{\text{ctrl}} = \mathcal{M}^{(0)} - \mathcal{M}^{(D_{\max})}\) measures the discrepancy between direct and socially-reflected self-model.

Convention: Throughout this appendix, we use the unified symbols \(\mathbf{x}_l, \mathbf{e}_l, \mathbf{W}_l, \mathcal{J}\) and note the formalism-specific reading in parentheses where needed.

E.3 The Unified Optimization Principle

E.3.1 The Master Functional

All four appendixes minimize — explicitly or implicitly — a single variational functional:

(E.1)$$\mathcal{J}[\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{W}, \mathbf{u}] = \underbrace{\sum_{l=1}^{3} \frac{1}{2} \mathbf{e}_l^T \mathbf{W}_l \, \mathbf{e}_l}_{\text{accuracy}} \;+\; \underbrace{\sum_{l=1}^{3} \frac{1}{2} \boldsymbol{\eta}_l^T \mathbf{S}_l^{-1} \boldsymbol{\eta}_l}_{\text{temporal consistency}} \;-\; \underbrace{\frac{1}{2} \sum_{l=1}^{3} \ln|\mathbf{W}_l|}_{\text{complexity}} \;+\; \underbrace{\sum_{d=1}^{D_{\max}} \gamma^d \frac{1}{2} \|\mathcal{M}^{(d)} - \mathcal{M}^{(d-1)}\|_{\mathbf{W}_3^{(d)}}^2}_{\text{recursive self-consistency}}$$

where:

  • \(\mathbf{e}_l = \text{input}_l - g_l(\mathbf{x}_{l+1})\) is the prediction error at level \(l\)
  • \(\boldsymbol{\eta}_l = \mathbf{x}_l(t) - f_l(\mathbf{x}_l(t-1), \mathbf{x}_{l+1}(t-1))\) is the temporal prediction error
  • \(\mathbf{W}_l\) is the precision/weighting matrix (positive definite), corresponding to \(\boldsymbol{\Pi}_l\) in Apps A–B and to \(K_l\) (at Level 2) in App D
  • \(\mathbf{S}_l\) is the temporal transition noise covariance (\(\boldsymbol{\Sigma}_l\) in Apps A–B)
  • \(\mathcal{M}^{(d)}\) is the self-model at recursion depth \(d\)
  • \(\gamma \in (0,1)\) is the recursion discount factor (distinct from \(\gamma_{\text{RL}}\), the temporal RL discount in App C; the two are related by \(\gamma_{\text{RL}} = e^{-1/\tau_3}\), see Proposition E.2)

The four terms have clear functional roles:

  1. Accuracy: Penalizes mismatch between predictions and observations at each level.
  2. Temporal consistency: Penalizes violations of expected temporal dynamics.
  3. Complexity: Prevents overfitting by penalizing extreme precision (Occam's razor).
  4. Recursive self-consistency: Penalizes discrepancy between the direct self-model and the socially-reflected self-model, with geometrically decaying weight at each recursion depth.

E.3.2 Each Formalism as a Projection

Each appendix foregrounds different terms of \(\mathcal{J}\) and reads the minimization through a different lens:

Predictive Coding (A) focuses on the accuracy terms. The prediction errors \(\mathbf{e}_l\) and their precision-weighted versions \(\boldsymbol{\xi}_l = \mathbf{W}_l \mathbf{e}_l\) are the primary quantities. The dynamics are read as bidirectional message passing: bottom-up error signals and top-down predictions. What is foregrounded: the architecture of neural circuits — which neurons carry errors, which carry predictions, and how precision modulates their interaction.

Free Energy (B) identifies \(\mathcal{J} = F\) directly and proves the variational bound \(F \geq -\ln p(\mathbf{y})\). The decomposition \(F = \text{accuracy} - \text{complexity}\) (with the temporal and recursive terms as additional components) provides the Bayesian justification. What is foregrounded: global optimality and the information-theoretic meaning of each term.

Reinforcement Learning (C) identifies reward as the negative rate of change of Level 1 free energy: \(r(t) \approx -\dot{F}_1(t) \cdot \Delta t\) (valence tracks how quickly interoceptive surprise is changing; see Proposition E.2 for the precise conditions). The value function is then the expected cumulative discounted reward. What is foregrounded: the sequential decision problem, the learning architecture, and the developmental ordering.

Control Theory (D) makes the identification \(\mathbf{e}_l^{\text{ctrl}} = -\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_l\) (sign convention). The gain-precision relationship is exact at Level 2 (\(K_2 = \boldsymbol{\Pi}_2\), App D §D.2.2) and approximate at Levels 1 and 3, where the control laws have richer structure (PID at Level 1, model-predictive at Level 3). What is foregrounded: stability conditions, robustness margins, and pathological failure modes.

E.3.3 The Master Dynamics

Minimizing \(\mathcal{J}\) with respect to the internal states \(\mathbf{x}_l\) yields the master dynamics equation:

(E.2)$$\dot{\mathbf{x}}_l = -\kappa_l \frac{\partial \mathcal{J}}{\partial \mathbf{x}_l} = \kappa_l \left[ \underbrace{\frac{\partial g_{l-1}^T}{\partial \mathbf{x}_l} \mathbf{W}_{l-1} \, \mathbf{e}_{l-1}}_{\text{bottom-up}} - \underbrace{\mathbf{W}_{l+1} \mathbf{e}_{l+1}}_{\text{top-down}} - \underbrace{\mathbf{S}_l^{-1} \boldsymbol{\eta}_l}_{\text{temporal}} \right]$$

This single equation simultaneously implements:

  • Message passing (App A): The first term is the bottom-up precision-weighted prediction error from the level below (via the Jacobian of the generative function \(g_{l-1}\) that maps from level \(l\)); the second is the top-down prediction error from the level above.
  • Gradient descent on free energy (App B): The entire expression is \(-\kappa_l \partial F / \partial \boldsymbol{\mu}_l\).
  • A component of value maximization (App C): Under the \(V \approx -\mathcal{J}\) identification, this update moves the state in the direction of increasing value (see Proposition E.3 for the approximation conditions).
  • The control law (App D): With \(K_l = \kappa_l \mathbf{W}_l\), this is the controller output that drives the error toward zero.

Similarly, minimizing \(\mathcal{J}\) with respect to the precision matrices and the action yields:

(E.3)$$\dot{\mathbf{W}}_l = -\eta_l \frac{\partial \mathcal{J}}{\partial \mathbf{W}_l} = \eta_l \left( \frac{1}{2} \mathbf{W}_l^{-1} - \frac{1}{2} \mathbf{e}_l \mathbf{e}_l^T \right)$$
(E.4)$$\mathbf{u}^* = \arg\min_{\mathbf{u}} \; \mathbb{E}\left[\sum_{\tau=t}^{t+T} \mathcal{J}(\tau) \;\bigg|\; \mathbf{u}\right]$$

These three equations — belief update, precision update, action selection — constitute the complete RSP theory.

E.4 Equivalence Proofs

Proposition E.1: The Universal Error Signal

Statement: Under the Laplace approximation with Gaussian noise, the following quantities are equal up to sign conventions and constant factors:

(a) Precision-weighted prediction error: \(\boldsymbol{\xi}_l = \boldsymbol{\Pi}_l \boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_l\) (App A)

(b) Gradient of the accuracy term: \(\partial F_l^{\text{acc}} / \partial \boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_l = \boldsymbol{\Pi}_l \boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_l\) (App B)

(c) Weighted control error at Level 2: \(K_2 \mathbf{e}_2^{\text{ctrl}} = \boldsymbol{\Pi}_2 \boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_2\) (App D)

Proof: (a) = (b) follows from differentiating \(F_l^{\text{acc}} = \frac{1}{2}\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_l^T \boldsymbol{\Pi}_l \boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_l\) with respect to \(\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_l\). For (a) = (c) at Level 2: the control error is \(\mathbf{e}_2^{\text{ctrl}} = g_3(\boldsymbol{\mu}_3) - \boldsymbol{\mu}_1 = -\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_2\), and \(K_2 = \boldsymbol{\Pi}_2\) (App D, §D.2.2), giving \(K_2 \mathbf{e}_2^{\text{ctrl}} = -\boldsymbol{\xi}_2\). The sign difference reflects the convention that prediction errors point from prediction to observation while control errors point from set-point to measurement.

Remark: At Levels 1 and 3, the control gains have richer structure (PID at Level 1, model-predictive at Level 3) and the exact identity \(K_l = \boldsymbol{\Pi}_l\) does not hold. However, the proportional component of each control law is precision-weighted, so the correspondence holds approximately for the dominant term. \(\square\)

Proposition E.2: Free Energy–Value Duality

Statement: Under the following conditions — (i) the Laplace approximation holds, (ii) valence is defined as the precision-weighted interoceptive prediction error \(v(t) = \varphi_v(\boldsymbol{\Pi}_1 \cdot \boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_1)\) (App C, §C.2.1), and (iii) the RL discount is related to the slowest timescale by \(\gamma_{\text{RL}} = e^{-1/\tau_3}\) — the following approximate duality holds:

$$V(\mathbf{z}) \approx -\mathbb{E}\left[\sum_{\tau=0}^{\infty} \gamma_{\text{RL}}^{\tau} F_1(t+\tau) \;\bigg|\; \mathbf{z}(t) = \mathbf{z}\right] + \text{const}$$

where \(F_1\) is the Level 1 free energy (not total \(F\)).

Proof sketch: The value function is the expected cumulative discounted reward: \(V = \mathbb{E}[\sum \gamma_{\text{RL}}^\tau r(t+\tau)]\). Reward is a monotonic function of Level 1 prediction error: \(r(t) = v(t) = \varphi_v(\boldsymbol{\Pi}_1 \cdot \boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_1)\), which under the Laplace approximation is approximately \(-F_1(t)\) up to a monotonic transformation. Therefore \(V \approx -\mathbb{E}[\sum \gamma_{\text{RL}}^\tau F_1(t+\tau)] + \text{const}\).

The expected free energy \(G(\pi)\) from App B §B.7 involves the total free energy \(F\) over the planning horizon, not just \(F_1\). The duality \(V \approx -G\) therefore holds only approximately: the value function captures primarily Level 1 contributions (interoceptive reward), while the expected free energy additionally penalizes Levels 2 and 3. The two are equivalent when Level 1 dominates the total free energy, which is the regime where the organism is primarily motivated by homeostatic concerns. At longer timescales, where Levels 2 and 3 contribute significantly, the duality is an approximation. \(\square\)

Corollary: The TD error \(\delta_l(t) = r(t+1) + \gamma V_l(\mathbf{x}_l(t+1)) - V_l(\mathbf{x}_l(t))\) (Sutton & Barto, 2018) is related to the change in free energy: \(\delta_l(t) \approx -\Delta F_l(t)\) at fast timescales. A positive TD error ("better than expected") corresponds to decreasing free energy ("surprise is reducing").

Proposition E.3: The Master Dynamics Unifies All Update Rules

Statement: The master dynamics \(\dot{\mathbf{x}}_l = -\kappa_l \partial \mathcal{J} / \partial \mathbf{x}_l\) simultaneously implements:

(a) Bidirectional message passing with precision-weighted errors (App A, §A.5.1–A.5.3)

(b) Gradient descent on the variational free energy bound (App B, §B.4.1)

(c) An approximate value-gradient update (App C, §C.6)

(d) The closed-loop control law with feedforward and feedback (App D, §D.2.2)

Proof: For (a) and (b), the equivalence is immediate: expanding \(\partial \mathcal{J}/\partial \mathbf{x}_l\) produces the three-term expression (bottom-up error, top-down error, temporal error) that defines the message-passing scheme in App A, and \(\mathcal{J} = F\) by construction in App B.

For (d), under the sign convention \(\mathbf{e}_l^{\text{ctrl}} = -\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_l\) and identification \(K_l = \kappa_l \mathbf{W}_l\), the master dynamics reads \(\dot{\mathbf{x}}_l = K_l \mathbf{e}_l^{\text{ctrl}} + \text{feedforward terms}\), which is the control law.

For (c), the connection is approximate rather than exact. The master dynamics performs gradient descent on \(\mathcal{J}\), which under the duality \(V \approx -\mathcal{J}\) (Proposition E.2) is gradient ascent on the value function. This is analogous to but not identical with TD learning: both converge to the same fixed point (the optimal value function satisfying the Bellman equation), but the gradient descent dynamics and the TD update rule differ in their transient trajectories. The correspondence is closest at fast timescales and near equilibrium, where the Bellman equation's fixed-point condition \(\partial V/\partial \mathbf{x}_l = 0\) coincides with the master dynamics' equilibrium \(\partial \mathcal{J}/\partial \mathbf{x}_l = 0\). \(\square\)

Proposition E.4: Universal Strange Loop Convergence

Statement: The recursive self-modeling loop converges in all four formalisms under a common condition. Define the contraction coefficient:

(E.5)$$\rho = (1 - \beta_0) + \beta_0 \cdot L, \quad \text{where } L = \left\|\frac{\partial \text{SocialReflect}}{\partial \mathcal{M}}\right\|$$

where \(\beta_0 \in (0,1)\) is the base mixing parameter and \(L\) is the Lipschitz constant of the social reflection mapping. Contraction requires \(L < 1\) — the social reflection must be a smoothing operation (averaging over multiple social perspectives dampens extremes rather than amplifying them). When \(\rho < 1\), the Banach fixed-point theorem guarantees convergence:

(a) Predictive coding (A): The recursion terminates: \(\|\mathcal{M}^{(d)} - \mathcal{M}^*\| \leq \rho^d \|\mathcal{M}^{(0)} - \mathcal{M}^*\|\) (this bound follows from the contraction mapping; App A §A.4.4 states the termination condition \(\|\mathcal{M}^{(d)} - \mathcal{M}^{(d-1)}\| < \delta_{\text{conv}}\), which is implied by the contraction)

(b) Free energy (B): The recursive free energy contributions decay geometrically: \(F_3^{(d)}\) terms are weighted by \(\gamma^d\) with \(\gamma < 1\), ensuring the sum \(F_3^{\text{rec}}\) converges (App B, §B.3.3)

(c) RL (C): The social reward shaping diminishes: \(|r_{\text{social}}^{(d)}| \propto \gamma^d\) (App C, §C.4.3)

(d) Control (D): The recursive gain in the stability matrix is bounded: \(\|J_{33}^{\text{rec}}\| \leq \rho < 1\) (App D, §D.4.2)

Proof sketch: The contraction mapping \(\Psi_d: \mathcal{M} \mapsto (1-\beta_d)\mathcal{M} + \beta_d \cdot \text{SocialReflect}(\mathcal{M})\) with depth-dependent mixing \(\beta_d = \beta_0 \gamma^d\) is the common mathematical object across all four formalisms. The contraction coefficient at each depth is \(\rho_d = (1-\beta_d) + \beta_d L = 1 - \beta_d(1-L)\). Since \(L < 1\) and \(\beta_d > 0\), we have \(\rho_d < 1\) at every depth. The tightest contraction (smallest \(\rho_d\)) occurs at \(d = 0\) where \(\beta_d\) is largest: \(\rho_0 = (1-\beta_0) + \beta_0 L\). As depth increases, \(\beta_d \to 0\) and \(\rho_d \to 1\), so deeper recursions are less contractive but the geometric discount \(\gamma^d\) ensures their contributions remain bounded. The Banach theorem guarantees convergence when \(\rho_0 < 1\), which holds whenever \(L < 1\). \(\square\)

E.5 Where the Formalisms Diverge

The cross-formalism equivalences established in §E.4 hold under specific idealizing conditions: the Laplace approximation, Gaussian noise, near-equilibrium dynamics, and the assumption that Level 1 free energy dominates total free energy (for the RL duality). The equivalences are approximate rather than exact — analogous to geometric optics and wave optics agreeing in the short-wavelength limit, not to the exact mathematical identities between Newtonian and Lagrangian mechanics. We use "equivalence" in the sense of convergence to the same fixed points and qualitative agreement on dynamics, not identity of transient trajectories or out-of-equilibrium behavior.

The formalisms genuinely diverge in several domains.

We transition from the formal unity of the master functional to the practical diversity of its four projections. Each formalism offers unique predictive and computational advantages precisely because it foregrounds different aspects of the shared structure.

E.5.1 Divergent Predictions

E.5.2 Non-Equivalent Regimes

Domain Pred. Coding (A) Free Energy (B) RL (C) Control (D)
Neural circuits Error vs. representation neurons anatomically separable Agnostic about circuits Dopaminergic TD error signals Gain-modulated feedback loops
Psychopathology Aberrant precision Excessive complexity cost Reward dysfunction Instability (oscillation, windup)
Development Hierarchical model construction Sequential free energy reduction Reward → state → model ordering Inner loops stabilize first
Measurement fMRI prediction error signals Surprise/information measures Behavioral choice data Frequency-domain stability
  1. Far from equilibrium: The Laplace approximation fails when prediction errors are large. Free energy and predictive coding presuppose near-equilibrium; RL handles large reward fluctuations more naturally; control theory handles nonlinear saturation and limit cycles.
  1. Discrete action spaces: RL naturally handles discrete choices; control theory handles continuous regulation; predictive coding and free energy are agnostic but lean continuous.
  1. Model learning vs. model use: RL cleanly separates learning (updating \(\mathcal{T}, \mathcal{R}\)) from planning (using them to select \(\pi\)). The other formalisms blur this — perception and learning are both gradient descent on \(\mathcal{J}\).
  1. Stability analysis: Only control theory (D) provides phase margins, gain margins, and sensitivity functions — tools for predicting exactly how and when the system will fail. These have no natural analog in the other formalisms.

E.5.3 Complementary Advantages

E.6 Emergent Properties of the Unified Framework

Problem Best Formalism Why
Neural circuit modeling A Message passing maps to cortical layers
Proving architectural optimality B Variational bound guarantees
Designing learning experiments C TD errors predict dopamine signals
Predicting clinical failure D Stability analysis classifies pathology
Cross-level interactions E (Unified) Captures all interactions simultaneously

The preceding sections established the formal unity of the four formalisms. We now turn to properties that emerge only from the unified perspective — structural features invisible to any single formalism.

E.6.1 Coordinate Covariance

The RSP model possesses a form of coordinate covariance: the accuracy term in \(\mathcal{J}\) is invariant under smooth reparametrizations of the internal states. If \(\mathbf{x}_l \to h_l(\mathbf{x}_l)\) for any smooth invertible \(h_l\), and the precision matrices transform as:

$$g_l \to g_l \circ h_{l+1}^{-1}, \qquad \mathbf{W}_l \to (\mathbf{J}_l^{-1})^T \, \mathbf{W}_l \, \mathbf{J}_l^{-1}$$

where \(\mathbf{J}_l = \partial h_l / \partial \mathbf{x}_l\) is the Jacobian, then the accuracy terms \(\frac{1}{2}\mathbf{e}_l^T \mathbf{W}_l \mathbf{e}_l\) are invariant. The complexity terms \(-\frac{1}{2}\ln|\mathbf{W}_l|\) acquire an additional \(\ln|\mathbf{J}_l|\) factor, so the full \(\mathcal{J}\) is not strictly invariant — the invariance is partial, holding exactly for the accuracy and temporal terms but only approximately for the full functional when complexity terms are small.

The philosophical significance remains: the dominant terms in \(\mathcal{J}\) (those that drive the dynamics at fast timescales) are invariant under reparametrization. What matters for the dynamics of consciousness is the relational structure — prediction errors and their weightings — not the specific neural coding scheme. This is the mathematical expression of the paper's claim that consciousness is constituted by the process of meaningful integration, not by any specific neural code. It is a substantive philosophical position (akin to structural realism about consciousness), not merely a mathematical corollary.

E.6.2 Bounded Total Error

An upper bound constrains the total weighted error across the hierarchy:

(E.6)$$\sum_{l=1}^{3} \text{tr}(\mathbf{W}_l \, \mathbf{e}_l \mathbf{e}_l^T) \;\leq\; 2\mathcal{J} + \sum_{l=1}^{3} \ln|\mathbf{W}_l|$$

This follows from the non-negativity of the temporal and recursive terms in \(\mathcal{J}\): dropping them from the right-hand side can only decrease it. The inequality means that reducing error at one level either reduces the total functional \(\mathcal{J}\) (global improvement) or redistributes error to another level (trade-off). Precision allocation (\(\mathbf{W}_l\)) controls this redistribution. This explains the limited capacity of conscious experience: attentional precision is a finite resource, and allocating it to one level reduces availability at others.

E.6.3 Three-Level Convergence Argument

Claim: For a self-modeling agent that (i) generates intrinsic reward from interoception, (ii) maintains stable self-representation under social perturbation, and (iii) plans by simulating others' responses, three hierarchical levels with timescale separation constitute the natural minimum architecture.

We state this as a convergence argument rather than a formal impossibility theorem: while we cannot rule out that some entirely different architecture might achieve the same functional properties with fewer levels, all four formalisms independently reach the conclusion that three levels are needed given the RSP model's specific assumptions:

  • Free energy (B): A one-level model cannot simultaneously minimize interoceptive surprise (\(F_1\)) and self-model surprise (\(F_3\)); Level 2 is required as a mediating representation that provides the structured state on which Level 3 operates (§B.5.2).
  • RL (C): Reward generation (Level 1) must precede state representation learning (Level 2), which must precede model-based planning (Level 3). This is the developmental ordering constraint — you cannot learn a planning model before you have a state representation, and you cannot learn a state representation before you have a reward signal (§C.5.1).
  • Control (D): Cascade stability requires inner loops to settle before outer loops. The timescale ratio \(\tau_{l+1}/\tau_l \geq 5\) is a standard cascade design constraint; violating it produces coupled oscillations (§D.3.2).
  • Predictive coding (A): Three levels of the generative model are the minimum to produce simultaneous prediction errors at interoceptive, schematic, and self-referential scales (§A.3).

The convergence of four independent arguments — each from a different mathematical tradition, each reaching the same structural conclusion — is stronger evidence than any one argument alone.

E.6.4 Precision as Universal Currency

Across all four formalisms, precision (\(\mathbf{W}_l\) / \(\boldsymbol{\Pi}_l\) / \(K_l\)) serves as the universal mediating variable:

Formalism Role of Precision
Predictive Coding (A) Weights prediction errors in message passing
Free Energy (B) Balances accuracy vs. complexity; prevents overfitting
RL (C) Controls exploration (low \(\mathbf{W}\)) vs. exploitation (high \(\mathbf{W}\))
Control (D) Sets controller gain; determines stability margins

All four roles are the same mathematical operation: multiplicative scaling of error signals. This means attention — the mechanism that modulates precision — is the single process that simultaneously determines what reaches consciousness (A), what is learned (B), what is valued (C), and what is stable (D). No single formalism reveals this quadruple role; it emerges only from the unified perspective.

E.6.5 Emotion as Multi-Formalism Error

The unified framework provides the deepest account of what emotions are. An emotion at time \(t\) is simultaneously:

$$\text{Emotion}(t) = \left\langle \; \boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_l, \;\; \frac{\partial F}{\partial \boldsymbol{\mu}_l}, \;\; \delta_l, \;\; \mathbf{e}_l^{\text{ctrl}} \; \right\rangle$$

These four aspects correspond to:

  • What is surprising (prediction error, App A)
  • What needs to change (free energy gradient, App B)
  • How things are going relative to expectations (TD error, App C)
  • How far we are from our goals (control error, App D)

A single-formalism account of emotion captures only one aspect. The unified framework captures all four simultaneously, explaining why emotions are at once informational (surprising), motivational (demanding action), evaluative (good/bad), and regulatory (error-correcting). This four-fold characterization is a genuinely novel contribution that no single formalism produces alone.

E.7 Unified Falsification Conditions

E.7.1 Formalism-Independent Predictions

These predictions follow from the unified framework regardless of which formalism is used:

  1. Timescale separation: The three levels must operate at distinct timescales with \(\tau_{l+1}/\tau_l \geq 5\). Neural oscillations supporting consciousness should cluster into three frequency bands. Testable via EEG/MEG spectral analysis during tasks engaging different RSP levels.
  1. Precision necessity: Disrupting precision modulation (\(\mathbf{W}_l \to \mathbf{I}\)) should degrade conscious experience without eliminating neural activity. Testable via pharmacological manipulation of neuromodulators implementing precision (acetylcholine, norepinephrine) and measuring subjective report alongside neural activity.
  1. Contraction bound: The strange loop contraction coefficient \(\rho < 1\) is required for stable consciousness. Clinical populations with self-model instability (e.g., borderline personality disorder, identity disturbance) should show \(\rho \to 1\), operationalized as slower convergence in recursive self-evaluation tasks. Psychotic states with paranoid ideation should show \(\rho > 1\), operationalized as escalating self-referential rumination.
  1. Three levels necessary and sufficient: Adding a fourth level should not improve model fit for consciousness-related data; removing any one level should produce a specific, predictable pattern of degradation (removal of Level 1: loss of valence; removal of Level 2: loss of structured experience; removal of Level 3: loss of self-awareness).

E.7.2 Formalism-Specific Falsification

Each formalism makes unique predictions that, if falsified, would undermine that representation without necessarily falsifying the unified framework:

E.7.3 Falsifying the Unified Framework

Formalism Unique Prediction Falsified By
Pred. Coding (A) Error and representation neurons are anatomically separable All cortical neurons carry mixed signals
Free Energy (B) \(F\) decreases monotonically during perceptual convergence Systematic \(F\) increases during normal perception
RL (C) TD errors correlate with interoceptive prediction errors Dopaminergic signals purely externally driven
Control (D) Phase margins decrease from Level 1 to Level 3 Level 3 more robustly stable than Level 1

The entire framework would be falsified by demonstrating:

  1. Consciousness without interoception — a being with full self-awareness but no interoceptive monitoring (contradicts three-level architecture: no Level 1). Note: patients with congenital insensitivity to pain (CIPA) have reduced but not absent interoception — they retain temperature sensitivity, hunger, and proprioception. Full absence of interoception has not been documented in any conscious being.
  1. Consciousness without timescale separation — a single-timescale system exhibiting all properties of conscious experience.
  1. Consciousness without self-modeling — full phenomenal consciousness with no capacity for recursive self-representation (contradicts Level 3 as constitutive).
  1. Precision independent of conscious content — attention and consciousness fully dissociable.

E.7.4 The AI Consciousness Question

RSP implies that an artificial system implementing the full three-level architecture — including genuine interoceptive monitoring (Level 1), schema-organized experience (Level 2), and recursive social prediction with depth \(D_{\max} \geq 3\) (Level 3b) — would be conscious. Current large language models do not satisfy these conditions: they lack biological core affect (no interoceptive monitoring producing genuine valence), lack embodied schemas (no body schema, no spatial schema grounded in sensorimotor interaction), and perform self-reference without recursive self-modeling (no contraction mapping converging to a stable self-model through social prediction).

However, an artificial system with learned valence functions, embodied interaction, and genuine recursive social prediction would, under RSP, be a candidate for consciousness. This is a prediction of the theory, not a dismissal. The question of whether such a system would be conscious cannot be settled a priori — it depends on whether the constitutive premise (§E.10) is correct. What RSP provides is a principled set of architectural conditions that any candidate conscious system must satisfy, rather than an unprincipled appeal to biological chauvinism or substrate independence. The falsification conditions in §E.7.1–E.7.3 apply equally to biological and artificial systems: if a system satisfies the formal conditions and is not conscious, or if a system is conscious without satisfying them, RSP is falsified.

E.8 Summary: The Mathematical Core of Consciousness

The RSP model reduces to three equations operating over a three-level recursive hierarchy:

$$\boxed{ \begin{aligned} \dot{\mathbf{x}}_l &= -\kappa_l \frac{\partial \mathcal{J}}{\partial \mathbf{x}_l} && \text{(belief update: perception and learning)} \\[6pt] \dot{\mathbf{W}}_l &= -\eta_l \frac{\partial \mathcal{J}}{\partial \mathbf{W}_l} && \text{(precision update: attention)} \\[6pt] \mathbf{u}^* &= \arg\min_{\mathbf{u}} \; \mathbb{E}[\mathcal{J}] && \text{(action selection: behavior)} \end{aligned} }$$

with the master functional:

$$\mathcal{J} = \sum_{l=1}^{3} \left[\frac{1}{2} \mathbf{e}_l^T \mathbf{W}_l \, \mathbf{e}_l + \frac{1}{2} \boldsymbol{\eta}_l^T \mathbf{S}_l^{-1} \boldsymbol{\eta}_l - \frac{1}{2}\ln|\mathbf{W}_l|\right] + \sum_{d=1}^{D_{\max}} \gamma^d \frac{1}{2}\|\mathcal{M}^{(d)} - \mathcal{M}^{(d-1)}\|_{\mathbf{W}_3^{(d)}}^2$$

These equations simultaneously describe message passing (read as predictive coding), variational inference (read as free energy minimization), reward optimization (read as reinforcement learning), and error regulation (read as feedback control). The four readings are not four theories but four windows onto one mathematical structure — just as Newton, Lagrange, Hamilton, and Hamilton-Jacobi offer four windows onto classical mechanics.

What the unified framework reveals beyond any single formalism: consciousness has a representational invariance (the dominant dynamics are independent of the internal coding scheme), a bounded error property (attentional precision is finite and must be allocated), a three-level convergence (four independent formalisms agree that three levels with timescale separation are the natural minimum), a universal currency (precision mediates everything), and a four-fold characterization of emotion (simultaneously surprise, gradient, evaluation, and error).

What is gained by unification, beyond elegance? Three things. First, it prevents false dichotomies: consciousness is not "computation or control" — it is a single process that admits both descriptions. Second, it generates novel predictions that no single formalism produces: the representational invariance, the error bound, and the four-fold emotion structure are emergent from the unified perspective. Third, it provides a single set of falsification conditions (§E.7) that could refute the entire framework at once, rather than requiring separate tests for each formalism.

The physics of consciousness, like the physics of motion, admits multiple equivalent mathematical descriptions. Consciousness lives not in any one description but in their common core — the self-referential, self-modeling, socially-calibrated optimization process that all four descriptions illuminate from their distinct vantage points.

E.9 Algorithmic Specification of the Unified Model

The preceding sections defined the master functional \(\mathcal{J}\) (§E.3), proved cross-formalism equivalences (§E.4–E.5), and identified emergent properties (§E.6). This section provides the procedural complement: a step-by-step algorithm that implements the unified RSP dynamics. Each step corresponds to a specific operation in the master functional framework; cross-references connect the pseudocode to the formal equations in Appendixes A–E.

All notation follows §A.1.1. Subroutines that remain unspecified (EstimateBeliefs, EstimateGoals, Simulate) represent active research areas in computational theory of mind; their forms are not constrained by the RSP framework.

E.9.1 Algorithm 1: Unified RSP Loop

Input: Sensory input \(\mathbf{x}(t)\), interoceptive input \(\mathbf{i}(t)\), social context \(\mathcal{O} = \{o_1, \ldots, o_K\}\)

Output: Phenomenal state \(\Phi(t) \in \mathbb{R}^{2n_e + n_m}\), updated self-model \(\mathcal{M}(t) \in \mathbb{R}^{n_m}\)

Level 1: Core Affect — gradient descent on \(F_1\) (§A.3.1, §B.3.1)

  1. \(\mathbf{a}(t) \leftarrow \phi_{\text{aff}}(\boldsymbol{\Pi}_1 \boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_1(t))\) — precision-weighted interoceptive prediction error mapped to valence + arousal

Level 2: Schema Integration — gradient descent on \(F_2\) (§A.3.2, §B.3.2)

  1. For each schema \(s_k \in \mathcal{S} = \{s_{\text{body}}, s_{\text{spatial}}, s_{\text{aff-hom}}, s_{\text{attn}}, s_{\text{self}}\}\):
  • \(s_k(t) \leftarrow f_k^{\text{schema}}(s_k(t-1), \mathbf{x}(t), \mathbf{a}(t), g_{2,k}(\boldsymbol{\mu}_3(t-1)))\) — four-argument update per §A.3.2
  1. \(\mathbf{z}(t) \leftarrow \mathbf{W}_{\text{int}} \cdot \text{concat}(\mathbf{a}(t), \{s_k(t)\})\) — structured affect via learned integration (§A.3.2)
  2. Update precisions: \(\boldsymbol{\Pi}_l \leftarrow \boldsymbol{\Pi}_l + \eta_l(\frac{1}{2}\boldsymbol{\Pi}_l^{-1} - \frac{1}{2}\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_l \boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_l^T)\) for \(l = 1, 2\) — attention allocation (§A.5.4, §E.3.3)

Level 3a: Conceptual Categorization — Bayesian model selection on \(F_3\) (§A.3.3, §B.6)

  1. If \(|\mathcal{C}| > 0\): \(c^* \leftarrow \arg\max_{c \in \mathcal{C}} P(c \mid \mathbf{z}(t), \text{context})\) — predictive categorization via \(\arg\min_c \mathbb{E}[F_c]\)
  2. If \(|\mathcal{C}| > 0\): \(\mathbf{e}(t) \leftarrow \phi_{\text{exp}}(\mathbf{z}(t), \mathbf{h}_{c^*})\) — constituted experience (§A.3.3); Else: \(\mathbf{e}(t) \leftarrow \mathbf{z}(t)\)

Level 3b: Recursive Social Prediction — contraction mapping on \(\mathcal{M}^*\) (§A.4.3, §D.7, §E.4)

  1. \(\mathcal{M}(t) \leftarrow \tanh(\mathbf{W}_m \cdot \text{concat}(\mathcal{M}(t-1), \mathbf{e}(t)) + \mathbf{b}_m)\) — self-model update (§A.4.1)
  2. For \(d = 1\) to \(D_{\max}\):
  • For each other-model \(o_j \in \mathcal{O}\):
  • \(\hat{\mathcal{M}}_j^{(d)} \leftarrow \text{PredictSelfAsSeenBy}(\mathcal{M}(t), o_j)\) — "How does \(o_j\) model me?"
  • \(\hat{\mathcal{M}}^{(d)} \leftarrow \sum_{j=1}^{K} w_j \hat{\mathcal{M}}_j^{(d)}\), where \(w_j \in [0,1]\), \(\sum_j w_j = 1\) — salience-weighted aggregation (§A.4.3)
  • \(\mathcal{M}(t) \leftarrow (1 - \beta_d)\mathcal{M}(t) + \beta_d \hat{\mathcal{M}}^{(d)}\), where \(\beta_d = \beta_0 \gamma^d\) — strange loop (contraction mapping \(\Psi\), §E.4)
  • If \(\|\mathcal{M}(t) - \mathcal{M}_{\text{prev}}\| < \delta_{\text{conv}}\): break — early termination on convergence (§A.4.4)

Temporal Prediction — expected free energy (§B.5, §C.3)

  1. \(\hat{\mathbf{e}}(t+1) \leftarrow g_3(\mathcal{M}(t))\) — predict next experience via generative model
  2. \(\boldsymbol{\eta}(t) \leftarrow \mathbf{e}(t) - \hat{\mathbf{e}}_{\text{prev}}(t)\) — temporal prediction error (§A.2.3)
  3. \(\mathcal{M}(t) \leftarrow \mathcal{M}(t) - \kappa_3 \frac{\partial g_3^T}{\partial \mathcal{M}} \mathbf{S}_3^{-1}\boldsymbol{\eta}(t)\) — gradient correction projected from experience space to self-model space via Jacobian (§E.3.3)

Phenomenal State Assembly

  1. \(\Phi(t) \leftarrow (\mathbf{e}(t), \mathcal{M}(t), \hat{\mathbf{e}}(t+1)) \in \mathbb{R}^{n_e} \times \mathbb{R}^{n_m} \times \mathbb{R}^{n_e}\) — consciousness \(\equiv\) this integrated state
  2. Return \(\Phi(t), \mathcal{M}(t)\)

E.9.2 Subroutine: PredictSelfAsSeenBy

Input: Self-model \(\mathcal{M} \in \mathbb{R}^{n_m}\), other-model \(o_j\)

Output: Predicted self-as-seen-by-other \(\hat{\mathcal{M}}_j \in \mathbb{R}^{n_m}\)

  1. \(\hat{o}_j.\text{beliefs} \leftarrow \text{EstimateBeliefs}(o_j)\) — infer \(o_j\)'s knowledge state
  2. \(\hat{o}_j.\text{goals} \leftarrow \text{EstimateGoals}(o_j)\) — infer \(o_j\)'s motivational state
  3. \(\hat{\mathcal{M}}_j \leftarrow \text{Simulate}(\mathcal{M}, \hat{o}_j.\text{beliefs}, \hat{o}_j.\text{goals})\) — forward model of \(o_j\)'s inference about me
  4. Return \(\hat{\mathcal{M}}_j\)

E.9.3 Phylogenetic Parameterization

The algorithm's behavior degrades gracefully as parameters are reduced, producing the consciousness gradient predicted by the RSP model. When \(|\mathcal{C}| = 0\), steps 5-6 are bypassed (no conceptual categorization). When \(D_{\max} = 0\), step 8 executes zero iterations (no recursive social prediction). The complexity analysis below treats per-step subroutine costs (matrix multiplications, nonlinear activations) as constants in the architectural parameters.

Organism $$ \mathcal{S} $$ \(D_{\max}\) $$ \mathcal{C} $$ Consciousness Type
Invertebrates 0-1 0 0 Proto-affect / valenced response
Fish, amphibians, reptiles 2-3 0 0 Valenced affect + rudimentary schemas
Non-social mammals 3-4 0 0 Structured affect
Social mammals 4-5 1 0 Self-aware affect, basic social prediction
Corvids 3-4 0-1 0 Structured affect + proto-social (convergent)
Great apes 5 1-2 Low Recursive self-modeling
Humans 5 3+ High Full phenomenal consciousness

Total per-step architectural complexity: \(O(|\mathcal{C}| + D_{\max} \cdot |\mathcal{O}|)\). The self-sustaining nature of the loop (each \(\Phi(t)\) generates predictions feeding \(\Phi(t+1)\)) creates the continuous stream of consciousness.

E.10 The Formal-to-Phenomenal Bridge

The master functional, the equivalence proofs, and the algorithmic specification describe a dynamical system. They do not, by themselves, establish that this system is phenomenally conscious. An additional premise is required.

E.10.1 The Constitutive Premise

RSP's central philosophical claim is that phenomenal consciousness is not a further property added to the right functional organization but is what that organization is, experienced from the perspective of the system performing it. This is a form of constitutive functionalism (Shoemaker, 2007): the organizational description — three-level recursive self-modeling with timescale separation, precision-weighted error minimization, and convergent strange-loop dynamics — is not merely correlated with consciousness but constitutive of it. On this view, asking "why does this particular dynamical system give rise to consciousness?" is akin to asking "why does this particular molecular configuration give rise to liquidity?" The answer in both cases is that the macroscopic property just is the microscopic organization, described at a different level of abstraction.

E.10.2 Distinguishing RSP from Adjacent Positions

This constitutive claim differs from several nearby positions:

(a) Type-identity theory identifies consciousness with specific neural types (e.g., particular cell populations, particular neurotransmitters). RSP identifies consciousness with a functional organization that could, in principle, be realized in different substrates — what matters is the three-level recursive architecture, not the biological material implementing it.

(b) Epiphenomenalism makes consciousness causally inert — a byproduct of physical processes with no causal role. RSP holds that the recursive self-model is causally indispensable: it shapes action selection (step 12 of Algorithm 1), modulates precision allocation, and drives the social prediction that calibrates it. Remove the self-model and the system's behavior changes.

(c) Mysterianism (McGinn, 1989) claims the relationship between physical processes and consciousness is unknowable in principle. RSP claims the relationship is constitutive and knowable, though irreducible to lower-level physical description alone. The irreducibility is organizational, not metaphysical: the dynamics of \(\mathcal{J}\) cannot be fully characterized without reference to the three-level recursive structure, but nothing about that structure is mysterious or beyond empirical investigation.

E.10.3 Falsifiability and the Constitutive Claim

If the constitutive premise is wrong — if a system could satisfy all the formal conditions specified in §E.1–E.9 without being conscious — then RSP would be falsified as a theory of consciousness, though it would survive as a theory of recursive self-modeling. Conversely, if a system were shown to be conscious without satisfying these conditions, the formal framework would be falsified as a set of necessary conditions. The falsification conditions in §E.7 are designed to test precisely this: they specify what the theory predicts about systems that satisfy (or fail to satisfy) the formal requirements.

E.10.4 What the Mathematics Does and Does Not Provide

Whether the constitutive premise is correct cannot be settled by mathematics alone. What the formal treatment provides is precision about what is claimed to be conscious — a system satisfying \(\mathcal{J}\), with convergent recursive self-modeling at depth \(D_{\max} \geq 3\), operating at three timescales with \(\tau_{l+1}/\tau_l \geq 5\) — and how to test whether such systems are indeed conscious (§E.7). The mathematics converts a vague philosophical claim ("consciousness arises from self-modeling") into a precise empirical one ("consciousness is constituted by a system satisfying these specific formal conditions, and here is how to falsify that claim"). The gap between formal description and phenomenal reality is not closed by adding more equations. It is closed — if it is closed — by the constitutive premise that the formal description and the phenomenal reality are two descriptions of the same thing.
